Dalalah al-Isharah from the Fasting Verse
Using the command to complete fasting to indicate that janabah does not invalidate the fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the ruling that janabah does not invalidate the fast from the Quranic verse via dalaalah al-isharah, using the Hanafi istinbat method.

Core Idea
The command to complete the fast implies it begins at dawn; permission for intimacy until dawn shows one may be junub, so janabah does not break the fast.

In this lesson, we examine a classic Hanafi example of dalaalah al-isharah — the inferred indication from the Quranic verse on fasting (Surah al-Baqarah 2:187). The text uses the verse to derive a ruling that may not be immediately obvious: that being in a state of janabah (major impurity) does not invalidate the fast. This derivation relies on combining two elements of the verse: the command to complete the fast and the permission for intimacy until dawn. We will walk through the logical steps and see how the Hanafi school uses isharah to produce certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two operative parts of the verse
Locate the permission for intimacy until dawn (أُحِلَّ لَكُمْ لَيْلَةَ الصَّيَامِ الرَّفَثُ إِلَى نِسَائِكُمْ) and the command to complete the fast until night (ثُمَّ أَتِمُّوا الصِّيَامَ إِلَى اللَّيْلِ). These are the textual anchors for the derivation.
The verse is from Surah al-Baqarah 2:187.
2
Determine the implication of the permission
The permission for intimacy continues until the break of dawn. Therefore, one may be in a state of janabah at the very moment dawn begins, as there is no required interval to perform ghusl.
This is a necessary consequence (luzum) of the permissibility.
3
Connect to the command to complete the fast
The command to complete the fast (أَتِمُّوا الصِّيَامَ) implies that the fast begins at dawn. Since one can be junub at that moment, janabah must be compatible with the fast; otherwise, the command would be impossible to fulfill.
The command to complete something presupposes its prior existence.
4
Derive the ruling via isharah
By combining the two implications, we deduce that janabah does not invalidate the fast. This is an inferred indication (isharah) from the text, not the explicit wording.
The Hanafi school considers this isharah to be decisive (qat'i), even if not immediately obvious.
5
Extend to madmadah and istinshaq
Since janabah is permissible during the fast, the acts required to remove it—rinsing the mouth (madmadah) and sniffing water (istinshaq)—are also permissible and do not break the fast.
This is a logical extension; if the cause (janabah) is allowed, the means to remove it are also allowed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿أُحِلَّ لَكُمْ لَيْلَةَ الصَّيامِ الرَّفَثُه إلى قوله تعالى: ثُمَّ أَتِمُّوا الصَّيَامَ إِلَى اللَّيْلِ ، فالإمساك في أول الصبح يتحقق مع الجنابة؛ لأن من ضرورة حل المباشرة إلى الصبح بالجماع أن يكون الجزء الأول من النهار مع وجود الجنابة، والإمساك في ذلك الجزء صوم أمر العبد بإتمامه، فكان هذا إشارة إلى أن الجنابة لا تنافي الصومَ، وَلَزِمَ من ذلك أن المَضْمَضَةَ والاستنشاق لا يُنافي بَقَاءَ الصوم. ويتفرع منه: أن من ذاق شيئًا بفمه لم يَفْسُد صَوْمُه، فإنه لو كان الماء مالحاً يَجدُ طعمه عند المضمَضَة لا يفسد به الصوم، ماء المالح من جنب من ماء المالح مع وجود الجنابة إلخ لأن كل جزء من أجزاء الليل وقت إباحة الرفث، وحل الجماع والغسل لا يمكن إلا بعد الفراغ عنه، والآن اللاحق بفراغه لا يمكن فيه الغسل أيضاً؛ لأنه أمر تدريجي زماني لا آني، بل له مقدمات تتقدمه تمياً أسبابه، فذلك الآن آن من الصبح ومن زمان الصوم، ويكون فيه جنباً لا محالة، فبهذه الضرورة ثبت عدم منافاة الجناية للصوم دليل قولنا: ما رواه الترمذي وصححه عن عائشة وأم سلمة مرفوعاً: كان يدركه الفجر وهو أهله، ثم يغتسل فيصوم، قال: والعمل على هذا أكثر أهل العلم من أصحاب النبي وغيرهم، وهو قول سفيان والشافعي وأحمد وإسحق الله انتهى، ثم علم أن الآية نص في إباحة المباشرة والأكل والشرب إلى ظهور انفجار الفجر وهو أول جزء الصوم، ومن ضرورته تحقق الصوم من أوله مع وجود الجنابة؛ لأنه لا واسطة أصلاً بين آخر جزء من وقت الإباحة وبين أول جزء وقت الصوم يتمكن فيها من الاغتسال كذا في "الفصول". بإتمامه حاصل المقام أن الأمر بإتمام الشيء مقتض لسابقية وجود الابتداء في ذلك الشيء، فيكون الأمر بإتمام الصيام مقتضياً لسبق ابتداء الصوم فكما أن الأمر يوجب الإتمام يفهم منه وجود نفس الصوم؛ إذ لا الأمر بإتمام المعدوم الأصلي كذا في بعض الحواشي. إشارة: قاطعة موجبة لحصول اليقين وإن كانت غامضة بحسب الفهم حيث لا يتبادر إليه بالبداهة عند مجرد سمع الآية لا تنافي الصوم لأنها لو كانت منافية للصوم لا يحصل الصوم في أول النهار وقد حصل، فلا تكون منافية له؛ لأن الشيء لا يحصل مع وجود المنافي كذا في "المعدن". لا يُنافي بَقَاءَ الصوم: لأن الجنابة لما تحقق مع الصوم ولابد من رفعها (أي الجناية) للصلاة وغيرها كسجدة التلاوة، وصلاة الجنارة، ودخول المسجد، وهي لا ترتفع بدون المضمضة والاستنشاق، الذين من أركان الغسل علم أنهما لا ينافيان الصوم كغسل سائر الأعضاء كذا في بعض الحواشي.
And similarly His saying: 'Permitted to you on the night of fasting is intimacy with your wives' up to His saying: 'Then complete the fast until night.' So refraining at the beginning of dawn occurs with janabah; because from the necessity of the permissibility of direct contact until dawn by intercourse is that the first part of the day is with the existence of janabah, and refraining in that part is the fast which the slave is commanded to complete. So this became an indication that janabah does not contradict fasting. And it necessarily follows from that that rinsing the mouth and sniffing water do not contradict the remaining of the fast. And it is derived from it: that if someone tastes something with his mouth, his fast is not invalidated; for even if the water is salty and he tastes its flavor when rinsing, the fast is not invalidated by it. The salty water from a junub from salty water with the existence of janabah etc. Because every part of the night is time of permissibility of intimacy, and the permissibility of intercourse and bathing cannot be obtained except after finishing it, and the moment after its completion cannot have bathing either; because it is a gradual temporal matter not instantaneous, rather it has prerequisites that precede it in preparation of its causes. So that moment is a moment of dawn and of the time of fast, and he is junub inevitably. So by this necessity, the incompatibility of janabah with fasting is negated. Evidence for our statement: what al-Tirmidhi reported and authenticated on the authority of Aisha and Umm Salama, raised (marfu'): 'The Prophet (PBUH) would be overtaken by dawn while he was in a state of janabah (from his wife), then he would bathe and fast.' He said: 'The practice according to this is the position of most of the people of knowledge from the Companions and others, and it is the saying of Sufyan, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, and Ishaq (may Allah be pleased with them).' Then it is known that the verse is a nass in the permissibility of direct contact and eating and drinking until the appearance of the break of dawn, which is the first part of the fast. And from its necessity, the realization of the fast from its beginning with the existence of janabah; because there is absolutely no interval between the last part of the time of permissibility and the first part of the time of fast in which one can bathe. As in 'al-Fusul'. By 'completing it', the conclusion is that the command to complete a thing implies the prior existence of the beginning of that thing, so the command to complete the fast implies the prior beginning of the fast. Just as the command necessitates completion, from it is understood the existence of the fast itself; because there is no command to complete something originally non-existent. As in some marginal notes. Indication: decisive, necessitating the attainment of certainty, even though it is obscure in terms of understanding, since it does not come to mind immediately upon merely hearing the verse. It (janabah) does not negate fasting, because if it were incompatible with fasting, the fast would not occur at the beginning of the day, but it did occur, so it is not incompatible with it; because a thing does not occur with the existence of a preventer. As in 'al-Ma'din'. 'It does not negate the remaining of the fast': because when janabah occurs with the fast, it must be removed (i.e., janabah) for prayer and other things like prostration of recitation, funeral prayer, and entering the mosque, and it is not removed without rinsing the mouth and sniffing water, which are among the pillars of ghusl. Thus it is known that they do not negate the fast, just like washing the rest of the body. As in some marginal notes.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of dalaalah al-isharah, now using the verse on fasting (Surah al-Baqarah 2:187). The verse allows intimacy on the night of fasting and commands completing the fast until night. The author argues that since the command to complete the fast implies that the fast begins at dawn, and the permission for intimacy extends until dawn, one may be junub at dawn. Therefore, janabah does not break the fast. This is an isharah from the verse. From this, it follows that rinsing the mouth (madmadah) and sniffing water (istinshaq) during ghusl are not incompatible with fasting, and likewise tasting something without swallowing does not break the fast. The text also provides a hadith from al-Tirmidhi to support this ruling. The author emphasizes that the indication is decisive and necessary, even if not immediately obvious. The commentary concludes by explaining that since janabah can coexist with fasting, the acts required to remove janabah (madmadah and istinshaq) are permissible during the fast.

Key Terms
جنابة (janabah): State of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl.
مضمضة (madmadah): Rinsing the mouth, a part of ghusl.
استنشاق (istinshaq): Sniffing water into the nose, a part of ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
From the fasting verse, what ruling is derived via isharah?
A) That one must be in a state of purity at dawn to fast
B) That janabah does not invalidate the fast
C) That madmadah breaks the fast
D) That one must bathe before dawn

Key Takeaway
The Quranic command to complete the fast and the permission for intimacy until dawn, taken together via dalaalah al-isharah, establish that janabah does not invalidate the fast.

Obligation Upon Commencement: Prayer During Disliked Times
Usul analysis of prohibition and commencement obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of 'al-luzum bi al-shuru' to derive the ruling for a voluntary prayer begun during a disliked time, and distinguish it from fasting on Eid day using qiyas.

Core Idea
A voluntary prayer begun during a disliked time becomes binding, but can be completed without sin by waiting; fasting on Eid does not bind because completion inherently involves sin.

This passage addresses a nuanced usul question: does the prohibition against praying at certain times affect the validity of a voluntary prayer that has already been started? The Hanafi school holds that once begun, a nafl prayer becomes wajib to complete, but the sin of praying in a disliked time can be avoided by pausing until the time passes. This distinguishes it from fasting on the day of Eid, where completion necessarily entails sin. The analysis involves applying the principle of 'luzum bi al-shuru' and performing qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
State the Hanafi principle that a voluntary act of worship becomes obligatory upon commencement (al-luzum bi al-shuru'). This is the core principle being applied.
This principle applies to nafl salah, sawm, and hajj.
2
Locate the Prophetic Prohibition
Identify the hadith cited: the Prophet forbade prayer at sunrise, zenith, and sunset. This prohibition makes the time 'makruh' (disliked) for beginning a prayer.
The hadith is from Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Apply the Principle to Prayer
If one begins a nafl prayer during such a time, the principle of luzum bi al-shuru' makes it binding to complete. However, one can avoid the sin by waiting until the disliked time passes (e.g., after sunrise) and then completing the prayer.
The sin is avoidable because the prohibition is on the time, not the act itself.
4
Distinguish from Fasting on Eid
Contrast with fasting on the day of Eid al-Adha: if one starts a nafl fast on Eid, it does not become binding (according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) because the entire day is prohibited for fasting; completing it would necessarily involve sin.
The distinction is that fasting is bounded by the day, while prayer is not time-bound in the same way.
5
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf holds that even fasting on Eid becomes binding upon commencement and requires qada if broken. This difference stems from whether the prohibition is seen as affecting the act's essence or its container.
The mufta bihi is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنه نذر بعبادة إلخ: أي يصح النذر بالصلاة في الأوقات المكروهة كما يصح النذر بالصوم في يوم النحر بأصله؛ لأنه يساوي سائر الأوقات في كونها ظرفاً صالحاً للعبادة، فاسد بوصفه وهو أي: صحيح أن الوقت منسوب إلى الشيطان كما جاءت به السنة؛ لأن النبي نهي عن الصلاة عند طلوع الشمس، ودلوكها أي زوالها، وغروبها، وقال : "لا يتحرى أحدكم فيصلي عند طلوع الشمس ولا عند غروبها"، وفي رواية: قال: "إذا طلع الشمس فدعوا الصلاة حتى يبرز، وإذا غاب حاجب الشمس فدعوا الصلاة حتى تغيب، ولا تحاذوا بصلاتكم طلوع الشمس ولا غروبها؛ فإنها تطلع بين قرني الشيطان".
وارتكاب الحرام ليس بلازم للزوم الإتمام، فإنه لو صبر حتى حلت الصلاة بارتفاع الشمس وغروبها ودلوكها أمكنه الإتمام بدون الكراهة وبه فارق صوم يوم العيد، فإنه لو شرع فيه لا يلزمه عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد بحملها لأن الإتمام لا ينفك عن ارتكاب الحرام.
وارتكاب الحرام إلخ جواب سوال مقدر، وهو: أن يقال: كيف يصح القول بلزوم الإتمام بالشروع، وفيه التزام : ارتكاب الحرام كذا في "الفصول". وبه فارق إلخ أي بما ذكرنا أنه يمكن الإتمام في الصلاة بدون الكراهة فارق الشروع في الصلاة في هذه الأوقات من الشروع في صوم يوم النحر ، فإذا شرع في الصوم في يوم النحر لا يلزمه الإتمام ، وإذا أفسده لا يلزمه القضاء في ظاهر الرواية، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يلزمه القضاء والإتمام في الصوم كما في وجه الفرق أن الصوم مقدر باليوم فلا يمكنه الإتمام بدون الإعراض عن الأكل والشرب في ذلك اليوم، وهو مكروه، فلا يمكنه الإتمام بدون الكراهة بخلاف الصلاة؛ فإنها لا توجد بالوقت؛ لأنه ظرفها، والظرف لا تأثير له في إيجاد المظروف بل الصلاة توجد بأفعال معلومة، والوقت مجاور لها، فلا يمكن فساد الوقت مؤثراً في فسادها فتدبر كذا في "المعدن".
Because he vowed an act of worship etc.: That is, the vow to pray during disliked times is valid just as the vow to fast on the day of Nahr is valid in its essence; because it is equal to all other times in being a suitable container for worship, and is invalid in its description. And it is valid that the time is attributed to the devil as the Sunnah has come; because the Prophet forbade prayer at sunrise, zenith, and sunset, and said: "Let none of you deliberately pray at sunrise or sunset." In another narration: "When the sun rises, leave prayer until it has fully risen; when the sun sets, leave prayer until it has fully set; and do not line up your prayers with sunrise or sunset, for it rises between the two horns of the devil." And committing a sin is not necessary for the obligation to complete; for if he waits until prayer becomes permissible – by the sun rising, setting, or reaching zenith – he can complete it without dislike. And this distinguishes it from fasting on the day of Eid; if one begins it, it is not obligatory to complete according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, because completion is inseparable from committing a sin. And 'committing a sin' etc. is the answer to an implied question: How can one say that completion becomes obligatory upon beginning, while it entails committing a sin? As in al-Fusul. And this distinction etc.: That is, by what we mentioned – that it is possible to complete the prayer without dislike – the beginning of prayer during these times differs from beginning the fast on the day of Nahr. If one begins fasting on the day of Nahr, completion is not obligatory; and if he spoils it, he does not have to make it up in the apparent narration. From Abu Yusuf, it is reported that he must make it up and complete the fast. The point of distinction is that fasting is measured by the day, so he cannot complete it without refraining from eating and drinking that day, which is disliked. So he cannot complete it without dislike, unlike prayer; because prayer is not defined by the time; time is its container, and the container has no effect on the existence of the contained. Rather, prayer exists by known actions, and time is merely adjacent to it, so corruption of time cannot affect its corruption. Ponder this, as in al-Ma'din.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the effect of prohibition on acts of worship, specifically voluntary prayer (nafl) during disliked times (mukruh awqat): sunrise, zenith, and sunset. The author first reaffirms that a vow to pray during these times is valid because the act of prayer is essentially legitimate, even though the time is disliked due to association with shaytan. The Prophet's prohibitions are cited. A key issue arises: if one begins a nafl prayer during a disliked time, is it binding to complete it? The answer is yes – the prayer becomes wajib upon starting (لزوم بالشروع), but one can avoid sin by waiting until the time passes (e.g., after sunrise) and then completing it without dislike. This distinguishes it from fasting on Eid day: if one starts fasting on Eid, it is not binding to complete according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two disciples), because completing it necessarily involves sin (since the entire day is prohibited). For prayer, the sin is avoidable by delaying completion until the time becomes permissible. The text also notes a difference of opinion from Abu Yusuf regarding fasting. The rationale is that fasting is inherently tied to the day (time-bound), whereas prayer is not; it can be performed across different times. This shows that the prohibition affecting the container (time) does not necessarily corrupt the contained (the act).

Key Terms
اللزوم بالشروع (al-luzum bi al-shuru'): The obligation to complete a voluntary act once started; it becomes binding (wajib) upon commencement.
الوقت المكروه (al-waqt al-makruh): A time when performing an act of worship is disliked (makruh tahriman or tanzihan) due to prohibition from the Prophet.
النهي (al-nahy): A prohibition; in usul, a request to abstain from an act. It indicates tahrim (unlawfulness) unless there is evidence to lower it to karahah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a nafl prayer begun at sunrise binding to complete, while a nafl fast on Eid is not binding?
A) Because prayer is more important than fasting.
B) Because the prohibition on prayer is weaker than the prohibition on fasting.
C) Because prayer can be completed after the disliked time passes without sin, but fasting on Eid cannot avoid sin.
D) Because Abu Hanifa changed his opinion on fasting.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of al-luzum bi al-shuru' makes a voluntary act binding once started, but this is qualified by whether completion can avoid the prohibition; prayer in disliked times is binding, fasting on Eid is not.

The Testimony of Sinful Witnesses: The Hanafi Usul Principle of Ahl al-Shahada
Ruling on the testimony of sinful witnesses with proof and application to li'an

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a prohibition (nahy) on a religious act implies the continued legality of that act, and derive the ruling on the validity of marriage with sinful witnesses.

Core Idea
A prohibition presupposes the existence of the prohibited act; thus sinful witnesses are still 'ahl al-shahada' despite rejection of their testimony due to corrupt performance.

This passage addresses a key usul al-fiqh question: Are sinful individuals (fussaq) considered qualified to give testimony (ahl al-shahada)? The Hanafi school answers yes, based on a rational principle regarding the nature of prohibition. Understanding this principle clarifies why a marriage contracted in the presence of sinful witnesses is valid, even though their testimony is not accepted in court. The passage then extends this reasoning to the institution of li'an (mutual imprecation).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage states that marriage is validly contracted with the testimony of sinful witnesses (fussaq). This implies that sinful individuals are considered 'ahl al-shahada'. Your task is to locate this ruling in the text and note that it is a direct consequence of the usul principle.
2
Understand the usul principle
The author invokes the principle: 'the prohibition of accepting testimony without testimony is impossible' (النهي عن قبول الشهادة بدون الشهادة محال). This means that for a prohibition to be meaningful, the prohibited thing must exist. Here, the prohibition of accepting their testimony only makes sense if their testimony is legally recognized as testimony in the first place. This is a specific application of the broader Hanafi principle that a prohibition on a religious act implies the continuation of the act's legality (nahy 'an al-fi'l al-shar'i yafdi baqa' al-mashru'iyya).
This principle distinguishes Hanafi usul from other schools that may treat prohibition as nullifying the act entirely.
3
Distinguish between capacity and performance
The text clarifies that the testimony of a sinner is rejected in court not because of a lack of capacity (ahliyya), but because of corruption in performance (fasad fi al-ada'). This distinction is crucial: capacity is inherent and cannot be removed by sin, but performance can be corrupted, leading to rejection in court. Identify where the text makes this distinction.
The phrase 'لعدم الشهادة أصلاً' is negated; the reason is 'لفساد في الأداء'.
4
Apply the principle to li'an
Li'an (mutual imprecation) is considered by the Hanafis as a form of testimony. Since the performance of testimony is corrupt in sinful individuals, they cannot perform li'an. Therefore, li'an is not incumbent upon them. Trace this application in the passage: 'وعلى هذا لا يجب عليهم اللعان'. Note that this is a direct consequence of the earlier reasoning.
Li'an consists of repeated sworn testimonies; corruption of performance prevents its validity.
5
Contrast with opposing views
The passage anticipates an objection: if they are ahl al-shahada, why is their testimony not accepted? The answer is given. Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) might argue that since their testimony is ultimately rejected, they are not ahl al-shahada at all. The Hanafi response distinguishes between existence of the legal capacity and acceptance due to performance. The practical difference (thamarah) is that according to Hanafis, a marriage with sinful witnesses is valid; according to the opposing view, it would be invalid.
The text explicitly addresses this objection with 'ثم يرد عليه أن الفساق لما كانوا من أهل الشهادة فينبغي أن تقبل شهادتهم فأجاب بقوله: وإنما لم تقبل إلخ'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أهل الشهادة، فينعقد النكاح بشهادة الفُسَّاق؛ لأن النهي عن قبول الشهادة بدون الشهادة محال، وإنما لم تُقبل شهادتهم لفساد في الأداء لا لعدم الشهادة أصلاً. وعلى هذا لا يجب عليهم اللعان؛ لأن ذلك أداء الشهادة ولا أداء مع الفسق. بشهادة الفُسَّاق: حتى لو تزوج رجل امرأة بحضور شاهدين فاسقين يقرر ذلك النكاح شرعاً حتى لو أتى العاقدان عند القاضي وأخبرا بالنكاح بشهادتهما وجب على القاضي أن يقرر ذلك النكاح؛ لأنهما من أهل الشهادة بمقتضى النهي بناء على أن النهي عن الأفعال الشرعية يقتضي بقاء المشروعية؛ لأن النهي عن قبول الشهادة بدون الشهادة محال؛ لأن قبول الشيء وعدماً إنما يتصور بعد وجود ذلك الشيء، فثبت أنه من أهل الشهادة. بدون الشهادة محال: لأنه لو لم يكن شهادتهم شهادة لا يتصور النهي عن قبولها، ثم يرد عليه أن الفساق لما كانوا من أهل الشهادة فينبغي أن تقبل شهادتهم فأجاب بقوله: وإنما لم تقبل إلخ كذا في "المعدن". وعلى هذا: أي على أن شهادة الفاسق غير مقبولة لفساد في أدائها. اللعان بيانه إذا قذف الرجل امرأته بالزنا، وهما من أهل الشهادة والمرأة فمن يحد قاذفها بأن كانت عفيفة من الزنا فطالبته بموجب القذف فعليه اللعان وهو في اللغة الطرد والبعد وفي الشرع هي شهادات مؤكدات بالأيمان مقرونة باللعان، وفي الغضب قائمة مقام حد القذف في حقه، ومقام حد الزنا في حقها، وصفة اللعان: أن يبتدئ القاضي بالزوج فيشهد أربع شهادات، ويقول في كل مرة: أشهد بالله إني لمن الصادقين فيما رميتها به من الزنا يشير إليها في جميع ذلك، ويقول في الخامسة : لعنة الله عليه إن كان من الكاذبين، ثم تشهد المرأة أربع مرات تقول في كل مرة: أشهد بالله إنه لمن الكاذبين فيما رماني به من الزنا، وتقول في الخامس: غضب الله عليها إن من الصادقين فيما رماني به من الزنا كذا قيل
They are among the people of testimony, so marriage is contracted with the testimony of sinners; because the prohibition of accepting testimony without testimony is impossible. And they were not accepted because of corruption in performance, not because of lack of testimony essentially. And on this basis, li'an is not incumbent upon them; because that is performing testimony, and there is no performance with sin. 'With the testimony of sinners': even if a man marries a woman in the presence of two sinful witnesses, that marriage is confirmed religiously; so much so that if the parties come to the judge and inform him of the marriage with their testimony, it is incumbent upon the judge to confirm that marriage; because they are among the people of testimony by virtue of the prohibition, based on the principle that prohibition of religious actions implies the continuation of legality; because the prohibition of accepting testimony without testimony is impossible; because the acceptance or non-acceptance of something is only conceivable after the existence of that thing. So it is established that he is among the people of testimony. 'Without testimony is impossible': because if their testimony were not testimony, the prohibition of accepting it would not be conceivable. Then it is objected against him that since sinners are among the people of testimony, their testimony ought to be accepted. So he answered with his saying: 'And they were not accepted' etc. Thus in 'al-Ma'din'. 'And on this basis': i.e., on the basis that the testimony of a sinner is not accepted due to corruption in its performance. 'Li'an': its explanation: if a man accuses his wife of adultery, and both are among the people of testimony, and the woman is one whose accuser is subject to the hadd punishment by being chaste from adultery, so she demands the consequence of the accusation; then li'an is incumbent upon him. Li'an in language is 'expulsion' and 'distance', and in religious law it is testimonies emphasized with oaths accompanied by curses, and in anger it stands in place of the hadd for slander with regard to him, and in place of the hadd for adultery with regard to her. The description of li'an: the judge begins with the husband, and he testifies four testimonies, saying each time: 'I bear witness by Allah that I am indeed among the truthful in what I have accused her of regarding adultery,' pointing to her in all that, and says in the fifth: 'The curse of Allah be upon him if he is among the liars.' Then the woman testifies four times, saying each time: 'I bear witness by Allah that he is indeed among the liars in what he has accused me of regarding adultery,' and says in the fifth: 'The wrath of Allah be upon her if he is among the truthful in what he has accused me of regarding adultery.' Thus it is said.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the legal status of testimony from sinful witnesses (shahadat al-fussaq). It establishes that such individuals are considered 'ahl al-shahada' (people of testimony) in the sense that their testimony has legal existence; thus a marriage contracted in their presence is valid. However, their testimony is rejected in court due to corruption in performance (fasad fi al-ada'), not due to any fundamental lack of the capacity to testify. The author uses the principle that a prohibition in religious law (nahy) implies the continued existence of the prohibited act as a legally valid act (baqa' al-mashru'iyya). For example, if Allah prohibits accepting testimony without testimony, that presupposes that there is such a thing as testimony from them; otherwise the prohibition would be meaningless. This is a usul principle. The author then applies this to li'an (mutual imprecation): li'an is a form of testimony, so if the husband and wife are sinful, they cannot perform li'an because it requires performance (ada') which is absent due to sin. The passage then explains the detailed procedure of li'an, which consists of multiple sworn testimonies and curses. This is a Hanafi position: that li'an is essentially testimony, not merely oaths. The text also notes that the rejection of the testimony of sinners is due to 'fasad fi al-ada', not because they are not 'ahl al-shahada'.

Key Terms
أهل الشهادة (ahl al-shahada): Persons qualified to give testimony; the text argues it includes even sinners because they have the basic capacity (ahliyya)
فساد في الأداء (fasad fi al-ada'): Corruption in the performance or delivery of testimony, which is the reason for rejection of sinful witnesses' testimony
اللعان (al-li'an): Mutual imprecation between spouses when husband accuses wife of adultery; treated here as a form of testimony (shahada)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the testimony of a sinful person (fasiq) not accepted in court?
A) Because they are not considered 'ahl al-shahada' at all.
B) Because of corruption in the performance (fasad fi al-ada'), not lack of capacity.
C) Because the Qur'an explicitly rejects all testimony of sinners.
D) Because they are not righteous enough to bear witness.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle that a prohibition implies the existence of the prohibited act establishes that sinful witnesses retain the capacity for testimony, even though their testimony is rejected for corrupt performance; this validates marriages contracted with them.

The Weakness of Prohibiting Zakat Value from Textual Silence
Critique of an inference from textual silence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and critique a flawed istinbat that infers a prohibition from a text's silence, using the Hanafi usul principle that silence does not imply prohibition.

Core Idea
A text that obligates an action does not automatically forbid alternatives; silence leaves room for substitution.

This passage examines a common istinbat error: using a hadith that prescribes a specific zakat animal to argue that paying its value is invalid. The author shows that the hadith is silent on the issue of value substitution, so it cannot be used as proof against it. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that a text's obligation does not imply exclusivity unless explicitly stated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proof being critiqued
Recognize that the hadith 'في أربعين شاة شاة' is used by some to argue that only a sheep can be given as zakat, thereby prohibiting paying its value.
2
Distinguish obligation from exclusivity
The hadith establishes the obligation (wujub) of giving a sheep. It does not address whether a substitute like value suffices. The issue is not the obligation itself but the method of discharging it.
The text is silent on the method.
3
Apply the principle of textual silence
In Hanafi usul, if a text is silent on a matter, it cannot be used to derive a prohibition. The silence indicates permissibility (ibahah) regarding alternatives.
4
Contrast the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions
The Hanafi school permits paying value, while al-Shafi'i prohibits it. The difference stems from whether the specification implies exclusivity. The Hanafi view relies on the text's silence.
This illustrates thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك التمسك بقوله عليه السلام: 'في أربعين شاة شاة' لإثبات عدم جواز دفع القيمة ضعيف؛ لأنه يقتضي وجوب الشاة ولا خلاف فيه، وإنما الخلاف في سقوط الواجب أي الزكاة بأداء القيمة. وإنما الخلاف فعند الشافعي رحمه الله لا يسقط، وعندنا إذا أدى قيمة الشاة مكانها يجزيء عن الزكاة؛ لأن الخبر يقتضي وجوب الشاة؛ لأن قول النبي عليه السلام وإن كان خبراً لكنه أكد من الأمر في الوجوب، ولا خلاف في وجوب الشاة، وإنما الخلاف في سقوط الواجب بأداء القيمة والنص ساكت عنه، فلا التمسك به؛ لأن النص لا يتعرض لعدم سقوط الواجب بأداء القيمة.
And similarly, adhering to his statement (peace be upon him): 'In forty sheep, one sheep' to prove the impermissibility of paying the value is weak; because it necessitates the obligation of the sheep, and there is no dispute about that. Rather, the dispute is about the fall of the obligatory duty, i.e., zakat, by paying the value. And the dispute: according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, it does not fall, while according to us, if one pays the value of the sheep in its place, it suffices for zakat, because the report necessitates the obligation of the sheep, and the statement of the Prophet, peace be upon him, even though it is a report, it is more emphatic in obligation. There is no dispute about the obligation of the sheep, but the dispute is about the fall of the obligation by paying the value, and the text is silent about it, so adhering to it is not valid, because the text does not address the non-fall of the obligation by paying the value.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents a second example of weak istinbat. The hadith states that for forty sheep, the zakat due is one sheep. Some argue from this that one cannot pay the monetary value instead; you must give an actual sheep. The author argues this is weak because the hadith only establishes the obligation of giving a sheep; it does not address whether paying its value is a valid substitute. The Hanafi school allows paying the value, while al-Shafi'i does not. The text is silent on the issue of value substitution, so using this hadith to prohibit paying value is invalid. This illustrates the principle that a text's silence on a matter means it cannot be used to rule on that matter.

Key Terms
سقوط الواجب (suqūṭ al-wājib): The fall/fulfillment of the obligatory duty (zakat) by performing an alternative action (paying value).
النص ساكت (al-nasṣ sākit): The text is silent; it does not address a particular matter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented, why is it weak to use the hadith 'in forty sheep one sheep' to forbid paying the value instead?
A) Because the hadith is not authentic according to Hanafis.
B) Because the hadith does not mention the permissibility of paying value, but it also does not forbid it.
C) Because the hadith implicitly allows paying value.
D) Because the hadith only applies to camels.

Key Takeaway
Textual silence on a matter cannot be used as evidence for prohibition.

Why the Riba Hadith Cannot Prove Non-Ownership in a Void Sale
Analyzing the weakness of using the prohibition of riba to prove a void sale does not confer ownership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the weakness in using a general prohibition hadith to derive a ruling on ownership, and explain the Hanafi position on the effect of a void (fasid) sale upon possession.

Core Idea
A prohibition does not automatically negate ownership; the text is silent on the legal effect of possession.

In this passage, we examine a Hanafi critique of a Shafi'i argument. The Shafi'i school uses the hadith prohibiting riba to claim that a void sale cannot transfer ownership. The Hanafi response is that the hadith only prohibits the transaction, but does not address the consequence of possession. This illustrates a key usul principle: the scope of a text's ruling is limited to what it explicitly states.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claimed Dalil
State the hadith cited by the Shafi'i school and the conclusion they draw from it regarding ownership.
The hadith is 'لا تبيعوا الدرهم بالدرهمين ولا الصاع بالصاعين'.
2
Distinguish Prohibition from Legal Effect
Note that the hadith explicitly prohibits the act of unequal exchange. The question is whether this prohibition also implies that the sale has no legal consequences (ownership).
The Hanafi position is that prohibition and legal effect are separate issues.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Rebuttal
Understand the Hanafi argument: the text is silent on ownership; it only prohibits the transaction. Therefore, it cannot be used to prove non-ownership.
The rebuttal rests on the principle that a textual ruling is confined to what the text explicitly states.
4
Examine the Shafi'i Counter-Analogy
Consider the Shafi'i analogy to theft: theft is prohibited and does not transfer ownership. The Hanafi response distinguishes theft (aggression) from void sale (consent).
The analogy fails because the context differs: consensual transactions may still produce effects despite prohibition.
5
Derive the Usul Principle
Conclude that a prohibition (nahy) indicates the act is haram but does not automatically nullify its civil effects. The ruling on ownership must come from another source or general principle.
This principle is often expressed as: 'al-nahy yufidu al-tahrim la al-ibtal'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك التمسك بقوله عليه السلام: 'لا تبيعوا الدرهم بالدرهمين ولا الصاع بالصاعين' لإثبات أن البيع الفاسد لا يفيد الملك ضعيف؛ لأن النص يقتضي تحريم البيع الفاسد ولا خلاف فيه، وإنما الخلاف في ثبوت الملك وعدمه. للشافعي دليله: أن البيع الفاسد حرام للنهي عنه، ولما كان المنهي عنه حراماً لا يصلح أن يكون سبباً لما هو نعمة وكرامة وهو الملك كالسرقة مثلاً، فإنها لا توجب ملك السارق في المسروقة، قلنا: إنه ضعيف بما مهدنا من قبل من أن النهي من الأفعال الشرعية يقتضي تقريرها، اعلم أن البيع الفاسد يفيد الملك عندنا بعد القبض، وعند الشافعي: لا يفيد وإن اتصل بالقبض؛ لأنه حرام، والحرام لا يصلح سبباً للملك الذي هو نعمة، لكنا نقول: إن النص المذكور يقتضي تحريم البيع الفاسد ولا خلاف فيه، وإنما الكلام في ثبوت الملك وعدمه، والنص ساكت عنه، فيكون ضعيفاً كذا في 'الفصول'.
And similarly, adhering to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams, nor a sa' for two sa'' to prove that a void sale does not confer ownership is weak; because the text necessitates the prohibition of the void sale, and there is no dispute about that. Rather, the dispute is about the establishment of ownership or lack thereof. Al-Shafi'i's evidence: the void sale is forbidden due to the prohibition, and since the prohibited thing is unlawful, it cannot be a cause for something that is a blessing and honor, i.e., ownership, like theft, for example, it does not give the thief ownership of the stolen property. We say: it is weak based on what we have established earlier that the prohibition of legal actions necessitates their establishment (i.e., the act still occurs). Know that the void sale does confer ownership according to us after possession, while according to al-Shafi'i: it does not, even if possession is taken, because it is unlawful, and the unlawful cannot be a cause for ownership which is a blessing. But we say: the mentioned text necessitates the prohibition of the void sale, and there is no dispute about that. Rather, the discussion is about the establishment of ownership or its absence, and the text is silent about it, so it is weak. Thus mentioned in 'al-Fusul'.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed example. The hadith prohibits exchanging unequal amounts of the same commodity (riba). Some argue that this proves a void sale (involving riba) does not transfer ownership. The author argues this is weak because the hadith only prohibits the sale; it does not address the legal effect of ownership after possession. The Hanafi school holds that in a void sale, ownership is transferred upon taking possession, even though the sale is sinful. The Shafi'i school argues that an unlawful act cannot be a cause of ownership, comparing it to theft. The Hanafi response is twofold: first, that the prohibition of an act does not necessarily negate its legal effects; second, the text is silent on the issue of ownership, so it cannot be used. This illustrates the principle that a text's prohibition does not automatically settle all related legal rulings.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay‘ al-fāsid): A voidable sale that is defective in some condition but includes consent and delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, why is the hadith prohibiting unequal exchange weak for proving that a void sale does not confer ownership?
A) Because the hadith is not authentic.
B) Because the hadith prohibits the sale but is silent on whether ownership transfers after possession.
C) Because the hadith explicitly allows ownership in void sales.
D) Because the hadith only applies to food items.

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of an act does not automatically negate its legal effects; the text must explicitly address the consequence.

Deriving the Option upon Emancipation and Talaq Numbers from the Barira Hadith
Derivation of two rulings from a single hadith using the causative fa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi derivation of the option upon emancipation and the rule of talaq numbers from the Barira hadith, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The fa in 'ملكت بضعك فاختاري' ties emancipation to option; Hanafi generalizes the cause, Shafi'i restricts it.

This passage shows how Hanafi scholars derive two interrelated rulings from a single phrase in the Barira hadith. Using the principle that the particle fa indicates causation, they establish that an emancipated slave woman has the option to annul her marriage regardless of her husband's status. Extending this logic, they determine that the number of talaq a husband can pronounce depends on the wife's status, not his own. This lesson walks through the derivation and the resulting inter-madhhab dispute with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the operative phrase and the fa
Locate the hadith phrase 'ملكت بضعك فاختاري'. Focus on the particle fa (ف) in 'فاختاري'. In Hanafi usul, fa indicates causation (al-sababiyyah), linking the preceding statement (ownership) to the following command (choice).
2
Determine the cause (sabab) from the phrase
The phrase 'ملكت بضعك' establishes ownership of sexual capacity (bida'). The fa shows that this ownership is the cause of the choice. The cause is the act of emancipation, which grants the woman ownership of her own sexual capacity.
The cause is the woman's ownership, not any other factor.
3
Generalize the cause to all emancipated slave women
Since the cause is ownership of sexual capacity through emancipation, it does not differ based on the husband's status. Therefore, the option applies whether the husband is free or slave. This is the Hanafi position.
The Shafi'i restrict this to cases where the husband is a slave, but Hanafis reject that restriction.
4
Extend the ruling to the number of talaq
The same underlying principle—that matters related to the woman's capacity (bida') depend on her status—applies to the number of talaq. The woman's status (free or slave) determines how many talaq her husband can pronounce. A free woman has three talaq; a slave woman has two.
This is derived by qiyas from the same hadith, as the text states.
5
Contrast with the Shafi'i reading
The Shafi'i school considers the husband's status for both rulings: they restrict the option to cases where the husband is a slave, and they tie the number of talaq to the husband's status (free = three, slave = two). This difference flows from a different usul approach to the hadith.
The Hanafi reliance on the fa as a general cause is the key difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا قال أصحابنا له : إذا أُعتقت الأمة المنكوحة ثبت لها الخيار، سواء كان زوجها عبداً أو حرَّاً؛ لأن قوله عليه السلام: "ملكت بُضْعَك فاختاري" أثبت الخيار لها بسبب ملكها بضعها بالعتق وهذا المعنى لا يتفاوت بين كون الزوج عبداً أو حراً. ويتفرع منه مسألة اعتبار الطلاق بالنساء، فإن بُضْعَ الأمة المنكوحة ملك الزوج، وعند أصحابنا : بحال المرأة، وعند الشافعي بحال الرجل حتى إذا كان الحر تحته أمة يملك عليها ثنتين عندنا، وثلاثة عنده، وإن كان العبد تحته حرة يملك عليها ثلاثاً عندنا وعنده ثنتين، ومذهبنا يتفرع على هذا الحديث. مسألة إلخ فعندنا العبرة بالنساء سواء كان الزوج حراً أو عبداً، وهو قول علي وابن مسعود وغيرهما، وعند الشافعي محله العبرة بالرجال والعدة بالنساء، وبه قال مالك في "الموطأ". * =, عن عائشة ما قالت كان في بريرة ثلاث قضيات أراد أهلها أن يبيعوها ويشترطوا ولاءها، فذكرت ذلك للنبي فقال: "اشتريها وأعتقيها، فإن الولاء لمن أعتق" قالت وعتقت فخيرها رسول الله ، فاختارت نفسها. أخرجه مسلم في باب إنما الولاء لمن أعتق رقم ١٥٠٤ ، وابن ماجه في باب خيار الأمة إذا أعتقت، رقم ۲۰۷٦ ، وأخرجه الدار قطني في كتاب النكاح بألفاظ مختلفة ،۲۰۱/۳، ۲۰۰، وأخرجه الدارمي في باب في تخيير الأمة تكون تحت العبد فتعتق.
And on this basis, our scholars (Hanafis) said: When a married slave woman is emancipated, she has the option (to annul the marriage), whether her husband is a slave or free, because the Prophet's saying 'You own your sexual capacity, so choose' established the option due to her ownership of her sexual capacity through emancipation, and this meaning does not differ whether the husband is a slave or free. From this branches the issue of considering talaq in relation to women... (then explains the Hanafi position that the number of talaq depends on the woman's status, not the man's, and cites the hadith of Barira).
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is an istinbat (derivation) of two rulings from the hadith of Barira, applying the principle that 'fa' indicates cause. First, the Prophet's statement 'You own your sexual capacity, so choose' uses fa to link the ownership (cause) to the choice (effect). Thus, whenever a woman gains ownership of her sexual capacity through emancipation, she gets the option to stay or leave the marriage, regardless of her husband's status—contrary to Shafi'i opinion that restricts it to cases where the husband is a slave. Second, the same principle of considering the woman's status extends to the number of talaq: a free woman has three, a slave woman has two, and this is tied to the woman's capacity, not the man's. The text presents this as a Hanafi stance derived from the hadith, referencing Imam Ali and Ibn Mas'ud, and critiques Shafi'i and Maliki views.

Key Terms
الخيار بالعتق (al-khiyār bi-l-‘itq): The option given to a slave woman upon emancipation to annul or continue her marriage.
اعتبار الطلاق بالنساء (i‘tibār al-ṭalāq bi-l-nisā’): The principle that the number of talaq is determined by the status (free/slave) of the wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view derived from the hadith 'You own your sexual capacity, so choose,' when does an emancipated slave woman have the option to annul her marriage?
A) Only if her husband is a slave.
B) Only if her husband is free.
C) Regardless of her husband's status.
D) Only if she was married before emancipation.

Key Takeaway
The fa in the hadith indicates a general cause, allowing the Hanafis to derive unconditional option and talaq numbers based on the woman's status.

Divorce Count Depends on Wife or Husband?
Hanafi proof that divorce count is based on the wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi proof for divorce count based on the wife and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-evidence using usul al-fiqh principles.

Core Idea
The number of divorces depends on the woman's status, not the man's, as the hadith on the slave woman proves.

In this lesson, we examine a classical dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i on whether the allowed number of divorces is determined by the husband or the wife. The Hanafis rely on a hadith where the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) states that the divorce of a slave woman is two, indicating that the wife's status (free or slave) determines the count. The Shafi'is cite a different hadith, 'Divorce is by men,' to argue the opposite. We will follow the Hanafi argument and their response, which involves questioning the authenticity or interpretation of the Shafi'i proof.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Clearly articulate that the number of divorces a woman can receive is determined by her own status (free or slave), not her husband's.
2
Identify the Hanafi proof
Locate the hadith: 'Divorce of a slave woman is two, and her waiting period is two menstrual cycles.' Note the use of the definite article 'al' (الأمة) and understand the Hanafi linguistic argument that the definite article indicates generality (lām al-jins), meaning the ruling applies to all slave women.
If the count depended on men, a free man married to a slave woman would have only two divorces, contradicting the principle that a free man can give three.
3
Present the Shafi'i position and proof
State that Imam al-Shafi'i holds the count is based on the husband, citing the hadith: 'Divorce is by men, waiting period by women.'
This hadith is narrated by Zayd ibn Thabit and others.
4
Explain the Hanafi response
The Hanafis argue that the Companions did not use the Shafi'i hadith in their discussions even though Zayd ibn Thabit was present, suggesting it is either weak, abrogated, or interpreted to mean only the act of divorce is by men, not the count.
Even if authentic, the hadith is interpreted (mu'awwal) to refer to the act of initiating divorce, not the number.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi
The Hanafi position is relied upon because of the clear, general hadith and the Companions' practice.
This is the operative ruling in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة اعتبار الطلاق بالنساء فيدار حكم مالكية الثلاث على عتق الزوجة دون عتق الزوج كما هو مذهب الشافعي. ولنا قوله عليه السلام: 'طلاق الأمة ثنتان وعدتها حيضتان'. وجه الاستدلال: أنه ذكر الأمة بلام التعريف ولم تكن معهودة، فكان اللام للجنس وهو يقتضي أن يكون طلاق هذا الجنس اثنين، فلو كان اعتبار الطلاق بالرجال لما كان للإماء ثنتان ولم يبق اللام للجنس. والجواب عن استدلال الشافعي بأن الصحابة تكلموا في هذه المسألة بالرأي وأعرضوا عن الاحتجاج بهذا الحديث مع أن راويه وهو زيد بن ثابت كان موجوداً فيهم، فدل ذلك على أنه غير ثابت أو منسوخ، ولئن ثبت فهو مؤول بأن إيقاع الطلاق بالرجال.
Issue of considering divorce with respect to women: the ruling of the triple ownership is made to revolve around the emancipation of the wife, not the emancipation of the husband, as is the madhhab of al-Shafi'i. And we have his saying (peace be upon him): 'The divorce of a slave woman is two, and her waiting period is two menstrual cycles.' The aspect of inference: he mentioned 'the slave woman' with the definite article 'al' while she was not previously known, so the definite article is for the genus, and it necessitates that the divorce of this genus is two. If the consideration of divorce were with respect to men, then slave women would not have two divorces, and the definite article would not remain for the genus. The response to al-Shafi'i's inference is that the Companions discussed this issue by opinion and refrained from using this hadith as evidence even though its narrator, Zayd ibn Thabit, was among them, indicating that it is not established or is abrogated; and even if it is established, it is interpreted that the effecting of divorce is with respect to men.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi proof in a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the basis for the number of divorces a woman may receive. The question is whether the count of divorces depends on the husband's status (free or slave) or the wife's status. Al-Shafi'i holds that the number of divorces is determined by the husband because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Divorce is by (the) men, and the waiting period is by (the) women.' The Hanafi school argues that it is determined by the wife, citing the hadith: 'The divorce of a slave woman is two, and her waiting period is two menstrual cycles.' The Hanafi reasoning: The Prophet used the definite article 'al' in 'the slave woman' (الأمة), which indicates the genus of slave women, implying that every slave woman has two divorces. If the count depended on the husband, then a free man married to a slave woman would also only have two divorces, contradicting the principle that a free husband can give three divorces. The Hanafis also question the authenticity of the Shafi'i hadith by noting that the Companions did not use it in their discussions despite the narrator (Zayd ibn Thabit) being present, suggesting it is either weak or abrogated. Even if authentic, they interpret it to mean that the act of divorce is performed by men, not that the count is based on their status.

Key Terms
الأمة (al-amah): A female slave who is married.
لام الجنس (lām al-jins): The definite article 'al' used to indicate the entire genus or species.
اعتبار الطلاق بالنساء (i'tibār al-ṭalāq bi al-nisā'): Consideration of divorce with respect to women; the principle that the number of divorces is based on the wife's status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what does the Prophet's statement 'Divorce of a slave woman is two' prove?
A) That the number of divorces depends on the husband.
B) That the number of divorces depends on the wife's status.
C) That a slave woman can only be divorced once.
D) That the waiting period for a slave woman is three menstrual cycles.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school proves that divorce count is determined by the wife's status using a clear hadith and linguistic analysis of the definite article, while questioning the authenticity or interpretation of the opposing evidence.

Deriving Tashahhud's Status from 'Aw'
Deriving the non-pillar status of tashahhud from the particle 'aw'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that the particle 'aw' indicates choice and derive that tashahhud is wajib, not rukn, from the hadith.

Core Idea
The particle 'aw' (or) in the hadith proves tashahhud is not a pillar—only one of two actions is required.

This lesson unpacks a classic Hanafi usul argument where a single particle in a hadith determines a major fiqh ruling. The passage shows how linguistic analysis (here, of 'aw') is used to derive the status of tashahhud in prayer, distinguishing between rukn and wajib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its operative term
Locate the hadith: 'If you say this or do this, your prayer is complete.' The key word is 'aw' (أو).
The hadith refers to the final sitting.
2
Understand the linguistic function of 'aw'
In Arabic, 'aw' typically indicates choice or alternation—completion is achieved by either of the two mentioned actions.
This is the Hanafi understanding: 'aw' does not require both.
3
Apply the Hanafi usul principle for 'aw'
The principle: 'aw' makes the completion conditional on one of the two, so neither is individually obligatory. Since sitting is a rukn by consensus, tashahhud cannot be a rukn.
This principle is specific to Hanafi usul; other schools may interpret 'aw' differently.
4
Derive the fiqh ruling
Conclusion: Tashahhud is wajib (obligatory but not a pillar). Omitting it requires sajdah sahw, but the prayer remains valid.
Contrast with Shafi'i view where tashahhud is fard.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Hanafi: prayer valid with sajdah sahw if tashahhud missed. Shafi'i: prayer invalid if tashahhud omitted intentionally.
This shows the real impact of the usul difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا قلنا: التّشهد ليس بركن في الصلاة؛ لأن قوله عليه: "إذا قلت هذا أو فعلت هذا فقد تَمَّتْ صلاتك "* علق الإتمام بأحدهما ، فلا يشترط كل واحد منهما، وقد التشهد والقعدة شُرطت القعدةُ بالاتفاق فلا يشترط قراءة التشهد.
* في الصلاة: أي في القعدة الأخيرة على طريق ذكر الكل وإرادة البعض كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَيَجْعَلُونَ أَصَابِعَهُمْ في آذانهم ، الآية. إذا قلت هذا: أي قرأت التشهد وأنت قاعد؛ لأن قراءة التشهد لم تشرع إلا في حالة القعود بأحدهما : أي علق الإتمام بأحدهما؛ لأن "أو " لتناول أحد المذكورين وهو القعدة أو قراءة التشهد، فيكون أحدهما فرضاً. فلا يشتَرِطُ إلخ وعند الشافعي الله ، فرض، وعند أصحابنا واجب حتى يجب سجدة السهو إذا عنه لكن الصلاة تتم بدونه لوجود أركانها؛ لما ذكرنا من التمسك بكلمة "أو" في الحديث.

* أخرجه أحمد في مسند الكوفيين رقم: ۱۸۲۲۷ ، بلفظ : "فإذا أتممت صلاتك على هذا فقد أتممتها، وما انتقصت شيء، فإنما تنقصه من صلاتك" للمسيء صلاته بعد أن علمه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كيفية الصلاة والطمأنينة ولم يذكر التشهد، وأخرجه الترمذي في باب ما جاء في وصف الصلاة رقم ٣٠٣ عن أبي هريرة منه، والنسائي في التطبيق رقم: ١٠٤٥٣، عن رفاعة بن رافع الله، وأبو داود في باب التشهد رقم: ۹۷۰، عن عبد بن مسعود ، والدارمي في الصلاة رقم: ١٢٩٥ بألفاظ مختلفة.
And on this basis we said: The tashahhud is not a pillar of prayer because his statement ﷺ: 'If you say this or do this, your prayer is complete' linked completion to one of them, so neither of the two is individually required. And indeed tashahhud and sitting—the sitting is required by consensus, so recitation of tashahhud is not required. * In prayer: i.e., in the last sitting, by way of mentioning the whole and intending part, as in the verse 'they put their fingers in their ears.' 'If you say this' means you recite tashahhud while sitting; because tashahhud recitation is only prescribed in the state of sitting. By 'one of them' i.e., completion is linked to one of them because 'aw' takes one of the mentioned things—either sitting or reciting tashahhud—so one of them is obligatory. Therefore it is not required etc. According to al-Shafi'i, it is a fard (obligatory), and according to our companions (Hanafis) it is wajib (necessary), so if one omits it, prostration of forgetfulness becomes obligatory, but the prayer is valid without it due to the presence of its pillars; because of our adherence to the word 'aw' in the hadith. ... Narrated by Ahmad ...
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a usul-based derivation of a fiqh ruling: that reciting the tashahhud (the testimony of faith) in the final sitting of prayer is not a pillar (rukn) of prayer, but rather an obligatory act (wajib). The proof is from a hadith of the Prophet ﷺ: 'If you say this or do this, your prayer is complete.' The hadith uses the particle 'aw' (or), which indicates that completion of prayer is achieved by fulfilling either one of two things: sitting for the final sitting (al-qa'dah) or reciting the tashahhud. Since sitting is agreed upon as a necessary component, the tashahhud is therefore not individually required. The commentary further clarifies that the hadith refers to the last sitting, and the mention of 'fingers in ears' is a common rhetorical device (mentioning the whole and intending a part). The ruling is contrasted with the Shafi'i school, which considers tashahhud a fard (obligatory). The Hanafis consider it a wajib, such that omitting it requires prostration of forgetfulness, but the prayer remains valid. The footnote at the end provides the hadith references, including Ahmad, al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, Abu Dawud, and al-Darimi, with variant wordings.

Key Terms
ركن (rukn): A pillar of prayer, the omission of which invalidates the prayer.
واجب (wajib): A required act that is not a pillar; omitting it does not invalidate but requires prostration of forgetfulness.
تشهد (tashahhud): The recitation of the testimony of faith (Ashhadu an la ilaha illa Allah...) in the last sitting of prayer.
أو (aw): The Arabic particle 'or', used here to indicate choice between two actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi usul principle used to derive that tashahhud is not a rukn?
A) The particle 'aw' indicates that both mentioned actions are mandatory.
B) The particle 'aw' indicates that completion is achieved by either action, so neither is individually required.
C) The particle 'aw' indicates that the second action is a replacement for the first.
D) The particle 'aw' indicates an exception to the rule.

Key Takeaway
The particle 'aw' is a powerful usul tool: it proves that tashahhud is wajib, not rukn, because the hadith makes completion contingent on either tashahhud or the sitting.

The Particle 'Fi' in Oaths: Acts Known by Their Effects
Explaining that acts are identified by their effects in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that an act is recognized by its effect in the locus to interpret the particle 'fi' in oath formulations, distinguishing between acts completed by the agent alone and those requiring an object.

Core Idea
Acts are legally named by their effect on the object; in oaths, 'fi' conditions focus on that effect's locus.

This lesson unpacks a key Hanafi usul principle: actions are legally identified by their effects on the object (mahall). The classical text from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani illustrates how this principle determines whether an oath is broken when the particle 'fi' appears in a condition. By examining different types of actions—insult, striking, and killing—we see how the effect-based rule operates consistently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
Locate the founding statement: 'Because an act is only realized by its effect, and its effect is in the locus.' This is the core of the analysis.
The principle is attributed to Imam Muhammad.
2
Apply to Insult (shatm)
Insult is completed by the agent alone. The condition for breaking the oath is the insulter's presence in the specified place, not the victim's.
The example: insulter in mosque, victim outside breaks oath.
3
Apply to Striking/Wounding (darb/shajj)
These acts require both agent and object. The condition is the object's presence in the specified place because the effect occurs on the object.
The striker's location is irrelevant.
4
Apply to Killing (qatl)
Killing is only realized at death. The condition is met if death occurs at the specified time, not when the wound is inflicted.
Death is the effect; the time of death governs.
5
Address the Counterargument
The objection that both agent and object should be considered is answered by noting that acts are named by their effects, which are specific to the object. Thus only the object's locus matters.
This reinforces the principle's consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في كلمة في
لأن الفعل إنما يَتَحَقِّقُ بأَثره وأَثَرُهُ في المَحَلِّ قال محمد يحلله في "الجامع الكبير": إذا قال: "إن شَتَمتُكَ في المسجد فكذا"، فشَتَمَهُ وهو في المسجد، والمشتوم خارج المسجد يحنث، ولو كان الشاتم خارج المسجد والمشتوم في المسجد لا يحنث. ولو قال: "إن ضربتك أو شجَتُك في المسجد فكذا" يشترط كون المضروب والمشجوج في المسجد، ولا يشترط كون الضارب والشاج فيه.
ولو قال: "إن قَتَلْتُكَ في يوم الخميس "فكذا فجرحه قبل يوم الخميس ومات يوم الخميس يحنث، ولو جرحه يوم الخميس ومات يوم الجمعة لا يحنَتُ.
لأن الفعل إنما إلخ لأن الأفعال إنما تعرف بظهور آثارها في محلها ألا ترى أنه تختلف أسماءها باختلاف آثارها، فإن من أرسل خشبة من الأعلى على غيره، فإن أثره في الإيلام يسمى ضرباً، وإن أثره في الجرح يسمى جرحاً، وإن أثره في إزهاق الروح يسمى قتلاً، ولما اختلف الفعل باختلاف آثارها علمنا أن اسم الفعل باعتبار ما ثبت بالمفعول به فيراعي المكان في حقه، ونحن نقول بهذا الاستدلال خرج الجواب عما قيل: إن هذه الأفعال تتم بالفاعل والمفعول لا بأحدهما، فيجب أن يراعي المكان في حقهما؛ لأن الفعل لم يثبت إلا لمعنى اختص بالمفعول وهو أثر الفعل، وهذا توضيح ما في "المعدن".
وأَثَرُهُ فِي المَحَلّ: أي في محل يقع عليه فيراعي المكان والزمان في حق المحل كذا في "المعدن". يحنث: لأن الشتم يتم بالشاتم ،وحده، فكان شرط الحنث وجوده في المسجد وقد وجد كذا قيل لا يحنث: وهذا مشكل؛ لأن تحققه كما يكون بما يؤثر فيه يكون بالمؤثر أيضاً، فكلاهما مما يتوقف عليه الفعل، واشتراط الظرفية في أحدهما دون الآخر تحكم؛ لأنا نقول: لما كان الفعل متعدياً وذكر المحل مع الفاعل يكون المقصود وقوع الفاعل على المحل ضرورة، ويكون المحل هو المقصود، فاشتراط الظرفية في المحل لهذا كما في "مفتاح العلوم". يشترط كون المضروب إلخ لأن الضرب والشج لا يتمان بالفاعل وحده، بل يتوقف على وجود المضروب والمشحوج أيضاً، فكان شرط الحنث وجودهما في المسجد. [عمدة الحواشي: ص ١٥٠] يحنَثُ: أي الحالف المتكلم بوجود الشرط وهو القتل في يوم الخميس؛ لأن القتل إنما يصير قتلاً عند زهوق الروح، ألا ترى أنه قبل زهوق الروح في يوم الخميس يسمى جرحاً، وبعد زهوق الروح يسمى قتلاً، فيراعي زمان زهوق الروح، و لم يوجد زهوق الروح في يوم الخميس، فلم يوجد شرط الحنث وهو القتل فيه كذا قيل.
Chapter on the word 'fi'. Because an act is only realized by its effect, and its effect is in the locus. Muhammad (al-Shaybani) said, analyzing in al-Jami' al-Kabir: If one says, 'If I insult you in the mosque, then (something),' and he insulted him while he was in the mosque and the insulted person was outside the mosque, he breaks his oath. But if the insulter was outside the mosque and the insulted person in the mosque, he does not break his oath. And if he said, 'If I strike you or wound your head in the mosque, then (something),' it is required that the struck or wounded person be in the mosque, and it is not required that the striker or wounder be in it. And if he said, 'If I kill you on Thursday, then (something),' and he wounded him before Thursday and he died on Thursday, he breaks his oath; but if he wounded him on Thursday and he died on Friday, he does not break his oath. 'Because an act, etc.': Because actions are only known by the appearance of their effects in their loci. Do you not see that their names differ according to their effects? For if someone sends a piece of wood from above onto another, if its effect is causing pain, it is called striking; if its effect is wounding, it is called wounding; and if its effect is taking life, it is called killing. Since the act differs according to its effects, we know that the name of the act is based on what is established in the object, so the place is considered with respect to it. We say by this reasoning the answer emerges to what was said: that these acts are completed by the agent and the object, not by one of them, so it must consider place with respect to both. Because the act is only established for a meaning specific to the object, which is the effect of the act. This is an explanation of what is in al-Ma'din. 'And its effect in the locus': i.e., in the locus upon which it occurs, so place and time are considered with respect to the locus. This is in al-Ma'din. 'He breaks his oath': because insult is completed by the insulter alone, so the condition of breaking the oath was his presence in the mosque, and it occurred. It was said: he does not break his oath; this is problematic because its realization occurs as much by what affects it as by the one affecting, so both are necessary for the act, and stipulating containment for one without the other is arbitrary. We say: since the act is transitive and mentioning the locus with the agent necessitates that the intended meaning is the occurrence of the agent upon the locus, and the locus is the intended object, so stipulating containment for the locus is for this reason, as in Miftah al-Ulum. 'It is required that the struck one, etc.': because striking and wounding are not completed by the agent alone, but depend on the existence of the struck and wounded person as well, so the condition of breaking the oath was their presence in the mosque. 'He breaks his oath': i.e., the swearer who spoke the existence of the condition, which is killing on Thursday; because killing only becomes killing at the moment of death. Do you not see that before death on Thursday it is called wounding, and after death it is called killing? So the time of death is considered, and death did not occur on Thursday, so the condition of breaking the oath—killing on it—did not exist. This is what was said.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a legal analysis of the particle 'fi' in oath formulations. The author presents a ruling from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani's al-Jami' al-Kabir: when an oath uses 'fi' with a place or time, the condition for breaking the oath depends on where the effect of the act occurs, not necessarily where the agent is. The principle is that an act is recognized by its effect in the locus (mahall). Thus, for insult (shatm), which is completed by the agent alone, the insulter must be in the mosque; for striking and wounding, which require both agent and object, the object must be in the mosque. For killing, the criterion is the time of death, not the injury. The commentary defends this against objections, explaining that transitive acts are attributed to the object of the act. This is a Hanafi usul principle: actions are named and legally assessed by their effects, influencing how conditions in oaths are interpreted.

Key Terms
أثر (athar): The effect of an act, which determines the act's legal name and attribution.
فعل (fi'l): An act that is legally attributed to its effect on the object.
محل (mahall): The locus or object upon which the act's effect occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, if someone swears 'If I kill you on Thursday' and wounds on Wednesday but death occurs on Thursday, what is the ruling?
A) He breaks his oath because death occurred on Thursday.
B) He does not break his oath because the wound was on Wednesday.
C) He breaks his oath because the act began on Wednesday.
D) The oath is invalid because killing requires intent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the particle 'fi' in oaths ties the condition to the locus where the effect of the act occurs, not to the agent's location.

Silence in Need Is a Statement: A Hanafi Usul Rule
Explaining the usul rule: silence in need equals statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that silence in a context of need is equivalent to a statement to identify consent or permission in legal scenarios, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Silence when clarification is needed is a declaration—the Lawgiver's silence permits, and a party's silence consents.

This rule from Usul al-Shashi is a cornerstone of Hanafi legal reasoning. It governs how we interpret the Prophet's silence on practices, the consent of a virgin in marriage, a master's permission for his slave to trade, and even the formation of silent consensus. Understanding its scope—when silence speaks and when it does not—is essential for sound istinbat.

Guided Path
1
State the principle
What is the core claim about silence in the context of need? Read the opening of the passage.
2
Identify the examples
List the four key scenarios: Prophet's silence on practices, shafi' silence, virgin's silence, master's silence on slave trading.
Each illustrates the same logic.
3
Examine the evidence
What proof does the Hanafi school offer? Consider the Quranic verses and the argument from necessity (harm avoidance).
The dalil is both naqli and aqli.
4
Analyze the dispute
What is Imam al-Shafi'i's objection and how do Hanafis respond? Map both positions.
This is a classic usul ikhtilaf.
5
Apply the condition
The principle is restricted to 'mawdi' al-hajah'. Explain why silence without need is not a statement, using the example of cutting the thief's hand.
This prevents overextension.
6
Connect to silent consensus
How does this principle justify ijmā' sukūtī as a valid source?
Shows the principle's broader role.
